                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.505,t=0.202,r=0.932,b=0.343), with ID 4d78bf6d-af51-485e-b4c2-53d8eae33ab1 -->

*C.7. Rate constants of transcription and translation ($k_8^{\mathrm{max}}$ and $k_4$)* <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.506,t=0.347,r=0.903,b=0.365), with ID 88a8e018-4109-4955-b293-2e791969dd62 -->

We assume that, given the temperature T, the transcription rate
constant k₃ᵀ may be approximated as a frequency of transcription
initiation of a single gene. Below, we use this assumption to es-
timate the upper bound of k₃ᵀ values, k₈ᵐᵃˣ. To estimate the fre-
quency of transcription initiation, we compute the ratio of poly-
merases speed during transcription to the distance between sub-
sequent polymerases. Analogously, we assume that the translation
rate constant k₄ may be expressed as ratio of ribosomes speed and
distance between them. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.506,t=0.374,r=0.932,b=0.492), with ID a54b7133-06ba-471a-b36d-29c780440896 -->

The range of transcription speed of a human mRNA polymer-
ase is reported as 600–6000 bp · min⁻¹ (10–100 bp · s⁻¹)
(Moran et al., 2010). Other sources report transcription elonga-
tion rate to be 3000–6000 nt · min⁻¹ (50–100 nt · s⁻¹) in HeLa
cells (Sehgal et al., 1976) and 1200 bp · min⁻¹ for hsp70 gene in
Drosophila (O’Brien and Lis, 1993). We assume the widest of the
above ranges, 10–100 bp · s⁻¹, which gives 600–6000 bp · min⁻¹.
Further, we assume that distance between centres of subsequent
polymerases on a DNA strand is not smaller than the polymerase
size. Literature reports that the calf RNA polymerase II in a DNA
footprinting assay protects 42 ± 2 bp (fully) or 51 ± 4 bp (partially)
of a DNA strand (Rice et al., 1993). We take the lowest of the men-
tioned values, 42 – 2 = 40 bp, into further consideration. To spe-
cify the upper bound for the distance between two polymerases, we
arbitrarily assume that it is 10³ times larger than the polymerase
size, i.e. 4 · 10⁴ bp. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.505,t=0.494,r=0.932,b=0.703), with ID 0f14795d-2804-468d-89a2-fa8fbed0c212 -->

$k_8^{\max} \in (1.5 \cdot 10^{-2},\ 1.5 \cdot 10^{2})\ \text{min}^{-1}.$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.506,t=0.705,r=0.931,b=0.788), with ID 65bac8dc-a568-4ad3-9a14-743e8db4d412 -->

The translation speed of a ribosome reported in Moran et al. (2010) ranges in 10–20 aa · s⁻¹. However, in Lodish and Jacobsen (1972), the average translation time of rabbit hemoglobin was measured as 0.94–1.491 s · aa⁻¹, which gives about 1.06–0.67 aa · s⁻¹. We assume a range covering all of these values, 0.5–25 aa · s⁻¹, which turns into 90–4500 nt · min⁻¹. The average spacing of ribosomes is reported as 25–40 aa, i.e 75–120 nt, for transcripts in Drosophila (Qin et al., 2007). Combining these ranges for the speed and distance of ribosomes, we get the following range for the translation rate constant:
k₄ ∈ (0.75, 50.0) min⁻¹.                                                    (C.7.2) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.506,t=0.791,r=0.931,b=0.941), with ID 91bdb426-6ac4-4b77-9024-1dbf05d661b9 -->

G. Dudziuk, W. Wronowska and A. Gambin et al./Journal of Theoretical Biology 478 (2019) 74–101 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.264,t=0.041,r=0.755,b=0.057), with ID 10dced6f-b500-4e37-b5f0-4b448e25c92f -->

91 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.928,t=0.042,r=0.950,b=0.056), with ID 95c03eea-0b2e-41d0-ac37-c3fad11c9d07 -->

C.8. Rate constant of constitutive HSP production ($k_6$) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.067,t=0.067,r=0.404,b=0.088), with ID 93116e1b-0e9f-4902-9c75-88b0da22c6db -->

To describe the constitutive HSP production rate $k_6$ in the reaction (2.12), we introduce the parameter $\epsilon_{\mathrm{HSC}}$, representing the fraction of bound constitutive HSP in homeostasis at $37^\circ$C. However, the model (2.1)–(2.12) does not represent constitutive HSP explicitly. Therefore, we introduce another auxiliary parameters: the total amount of constitutive HSP and the amount of free constitutive HSP in homeostasis at $37^\circ$C, which we denote by $\mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}}$ and $\mathrm{HSC}$, respectively. Given these, we assume the following relation:

$ \mathrm{HSC} = \left( 1 - \epsilon_{\mathrm{HSC}} \right) \cdot \mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}}. $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.066,t=0.095,r=0.500,b=0.224), with ID 2d7554fb-6aa9-4449-b6f6-796ba52b882e -->

Assuming that HSC\(_{tot}\) is constant (see also the discussion in Section 2) and stems from the balance between production (rate k\(_6\)) and degradation (rate constant k\(_9\)) of constitutive HSP, we make the following assignment: <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.067,t=0.224,r=0.499,b=0.280), with ID 9ff498c7-e943-49f6-b433-4e5bf2868811 -->

$k_6 = k_9 \cdot \mathrm{HSC} = k_9 \cdot \left( 1 - \epsilon_{\mathrm{HSC}} \right) \cdot \mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}}. \hspace{2cm} \text{(C.8.1)}$

The ranges of values of $k_9$, $\epsilon_{\mathrm{HSC}}$ and $\mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}}$ are given by Eqs. (C.5.1), (C.15.3) and (C.20.3), respectively. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.067,t=0.281,r=0.500,b=0.333), with ID 7af0a62d-163f-4822-b780-0b5aacde52c8 -->

C.9. Rate constant of HSE:HSF$_3$ dissociation (1$^{T}$) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.068,t=0.337,r=0.365,b=0.360), with ID fcca6b81-fad6-4596-ac19-2725dccb4c29 -->

Herbomel et al. (2013) estimated the dissociation rate constant for HSF1 located in a proximity of DNA to be 45 s⁻¹ before and 25 s⁻¹ after one-hour 43°C heat shock using the mFCS assay (Herbomel et al., 2013, Table 2), or to be 0.027 s⁻¹ after one-hour of a 43°C heat shock using the FRAP assay (Herbomel et al., 2013, Table 3). Authors suggest that the higher values of dissociation rate constant obtained with mFCS analysis may correspond to HSF1 weak interactions with chromatin while underestimating tight binding interactions of HSF1. Conversely, the low dissociation rate constant value obtained in the FRAP analysis captures these tight interactions, including the HSF1-DNA binding. Consequently, we take 0.027 s⁻¹ as a reference value for estimation of the rate constant of HSE:HSF complexes dissociation, $I_T^r$, for all temperatures T for which we define $I_T^r$, i.e. T ∈ [37, 45]°C. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.067,t=0.367,r=0.500,b=0.552), with ID 4da2495f-9cb4-44df-b27e-95462246beef -->

The FRAP assay (as well as mFSC) does not distinguish be-
tween specific DNA binding sites, effectively describing average
DNA binding interaction. Moreover, authors used an idealistic (con-
tinuous and space-homogeneous) mathematical model of bind-
ing to estimate the dissociation rate. In addition, since the men-
tioned mFCS-based estimates for dissociation rate constant depend
on temperature, we assume that the dissociation of tight binding
interactions assessed from FRAP acquisition also may depend on
temperature (see the discussion in Section 2). Therefore, we as-
sume for $I_T^1$ a large range of two orders of magnitude with respect
to the reference value, i.e. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.067,t=0.552,r=0.500,b=0.697), with ID 5287585c-2be8-4e51-b6e4-75263257d57c -->

$|I_{7}^{T}| \in (1.62 \cdot 10^{-2}, 1.62 \cdot 10^{2}) \ \text{min}^{-1}, \quad \text{for } T \in [37, 45]^\circ \text{C}.$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.068,t=0.698,r=0.471,b=0.719), with ID eb7e8566-0ac1-4f92-b7bf-4e2caf5a9932 -->

This implies analogous constraints for the parameters of $I_j^T$, which are just $I_j^T$ values at specific temperatures within the range T ∈ [37, 45]°C (see Section 2); <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.068,t=0.720,r=0.498,b=0.763), with ID c9c1f25b-6079-4261-96dc-ae9b458621e2 -->

$ I_{T}^{\gamma} \in (1.62 \cdot 10^{-2},\, 1.62 \cdot 10^{2})\ \text{min}^{-1},\quad \text{for}\ T = 37,\, 41,\, 42,\, 43,\, 44,\, 45\,^\circ\text{C}. \tag{C.9.1} $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.069,t=0.763,r=0.497,b=0.801), with ID ae2cdc5b-24ab-4999-a772-7e7d12e43712 -->

C.10. *HSE:HSF$_3$ dissociation constant at physiological temperature* ($K_{d, 37^\circ C}$) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.069,t=0.807,r=0.473,b=0.838), with ID e35185c3-7a57-4de5-8e59-ff85b38743ee -->

We introduce an additional parameter, the dissociation constant  
$K_{d,7}^T$, for the reversible reaction (2.2). We assume that the value of  
$k_7$ is assigned via

$k_7 = I_7^T / K_{d,7}^T.$  (C.10.1) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.069,t=0.846,r=0.498,b=0.909), with ID b5b436f1-76b3-4899-9db8-0c2360c000c7 -->

Since $I_7^T$ depends on T and $k_7$ is constant w.r.t. T in our model,
it is necessary to assume that $K_{d,7}^T$ depends on T, hence we use <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.068,t=0.912,r=0.498,b=0.943), with ID 6727f1a8-c9d4-45b0-a760-a398cab30046 -->

the superscript “T”. Now, we describe bounds for $K_{d,7}^{37^\circ C}$
assumed in this work. We base on in vitro study in homeostasis
at physiological temperature of the HSE and HSF affinity for HSF
engineered from a mouse DNA (Kroeger et al., 1993). The mea-
surements of this study are presented as a Scatchard plot on Fig-
ure 2 in Kroeger et al. (1993). We fitted a linear model to that
Scatchard plot and assumed the reciprocal of its slope, 2.71 $\mu$M, to
be a reference value for $K_{d,7}^{37^\circ C}$. This value is in agreement with the
value computed in Kroeger et al. (1993). To estimate error for this
value, we computed maximal and minimal negative slopes pass-
ing through the line contained within the distance of standard de-
viation of residuals from the plot of the fitted linear model. We
assumed that the error for $K_{d,7}^{37^\circ C}$ should cover reciprocals of the ob-
tained maximal and minimal slopes. Since the Scatchard plot con-
tains error in both axes, we next swapped the role of the axes and
performed the analogous procedure of fitting a linear model and
computing maximal and minimal negative slopes. We further as-
sumed that these maximal and minimal slopes should be covered
by the error for $K_{d,7}^{37^\circ C}$. It turned out that the above assumptions are
satisfied with 3-fold error imposed for the reference value, hence
we assume that <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.518,t=0.070,r=0.951,b=0.347), with ID 7e6026b3-cae9-44d0-ae5c-04453ff452be -->

$K_{d,7}^{T} \in \left(\frac{1}{3} \cdot 2.71 \cdot 10^{-3},\ 3 \cdot 2.71 \cdot 10^{-3}\right)\ \mu\mathrm{M},\quad \text{for}\ T = 37^\circ\mathrm{C}.\qquad\text{(C.10.2)}$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.521,t=0.353,r=0.950,b=0.394), with ID ffb052ac-f3e7-4c0f-9cba-7a3937bfa5ab -->

*C.11. HSP:HSF dissociation constant (K<sub>d,2</sub>)*

For the reversible reaction (2.6), we introduce the dissociation constant K<sub>d,2</sub> and assume that the value of I<sub>2</sub> is given by the assignment

$I_2 = K_{d,2} \cdot k_2.$

(C.11.1) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.520,t=0.402,r=0.950,b=0.494), with ID 8b911922-b346-42bc-bb43-490a61dfd593 -->

To estimate bounds for the dissociation constant K\(_{d,2}\), we investigate the equilibrium of the reaction (2.6), isolated from the model (2.1)–(2.12). We have <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.521,t=0.498,r=0.949,b=0.539), with ID 7a2325bf-7e79-4bfb-9506-699321d996d2 -->

$K_{d,2} = \frac{\mathrm{HSP}_* \cdot \mathrm{HSF}_*}{\mathrm{HSP:HSF}_*},$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.521,t=0.543,r=0.950,b=0.644), with ID 344b0f14-19a0-4068-aa70-4f0b9a52f1fa -->

$K_{\mathrm{d},2} = \frac{\mathrm{HSP}_* \cdot \alpha_{\mathrm{free}} \cdot \mathrm{HSF}_\Sigma}{(1-\alpha_{\mathrm{free}}) \cdot \mathrm{HSF}_\Sigma} = \frac{\alpha_{\mathrm{free}}}{1-\alpha_{\mathrm{free}}} \cdot \mathrm{HSP}_*.$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.522,t=0.646,r=0.838,b=0.679), with ID ca329ad1-128f-47a1-912f-db2f7ae0632f -->

Now, assume that:

(*) Concentrations HSP$_\Sigma$ and HSF$_\Sigma$ in the isolated reaction (2.6) are equal to corresponding concentrations in the model (2.1)–(2.12) with T = 37°C.

(**) The fraction $\alpha_{free}$ of free HSF in the equilibrium of the isolated reaction (2.6) may be approximated by the fraction of free HSF in the equilibrium of the model (2.1)–(2.12) with T = 37°C. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.521,t=0.681,r=0.950,b=0.798), with ID 4875ab1b-c863-4e02-88e5-c793d98ef0c0 -->

Note that the assumption (**) is non-trivial since the equilibrium
concentrations of HSP, HSF and HSP:HSF in the isolated reaction
(2.6) and in the whole model (2.1)–(2.12) may differ. We estimate
the fraction of free HSF in the model (2.1)–(2.12) with T = 37°C as
smaller than 10%, see Eq. (C.12.4), thus by the assumption (**), we
have <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.521,t=0.807,r=0.950,b=0.886), with ID 43b9c8ab-9fa3-4216-aefb-fde9e619337e -->

$\alpha_\mathrm{free} < 0.1.$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.522,t=0.893,r=0.598,b=0.908), with ID 08905c5c-dbc1-48ea-aa07-a2ad91a9222c -->

Moreover, we estimate HSP• from above by HSPΣ and we estimate
HSPΣ by the total HSP concentration in the model (2.1)–(2.12) with <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.522,t=0.914,r=0.948,b=0.942), with ID 0277bece-969a-400d-9e2e-e41ea116554b -->

92 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.050,t=0.043,r=0.072,b=0.055), with ID 59985a10-93c2-4559-bc1a-b4512dd2343b -->

G. Dudziuk, W. Wronowska and A. Gambin et al./Journal of Theoretical Biology 478 (2019) 74–101 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.249,t=0.043,r=0.737,b=0.057), with ID 6f425a8c-56b4-4cd5-a5a4-1fb398677775 -->

T = 37°C, which is possible by the assumption (*). Using the upper
bound given by Eq. (C.20.2), we have <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.050,t=0.069,r=0.483,b=0.099), with ID 4656b042-f3b3-48ba-8a25-721218cad11d -->

$ \text{HSP}_* < 2 \cdot 28.5486 \ \mu \text{M}. $

To sum up, <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.051,t=0.102,r=0.227,b=0.139), with ID 4373c679-a0e7-47d0-b5a5-87f15e6044c3 -->

$K_{\mathrm{d},2} \leq \frac{0.1}{1-0.1} \cdot 2 \cdot 28.5486\ \mu\mathrm{M},$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.142,r=0.294,b=0.174), with ID 9f470ae7-9234-4add-a487-bfbf1c4b0ae5 -->

which gives the following range for Kd,2:

Kd,2 ∈ (0, 6.3441)   μM.                                            (C.11.2) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.176,r=0.481,b=0.218), with ID 770d1cac-463f-4a8c-a869-73df153b8c5d -->

C.12. Fractions of free HSF in homeostasis at 37°C

Let HSF$_{\text{tot}}^{\text{free}}$ denote the total amount of free cellular HSF (not bound either as a monomer to HSP nor as a trimmer to HSE) in homeostasis at T = 37°C: <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.051,t=0.226,r=0.482,b=0.295), with ID fdbb48d7-26b1-4cf1-83aa-c86d7494ba68 -->

$ \mathrm{HSF}_{\mathrm{tot}}^{\mathrm{free}} := \mathrm{HSF0} + 3 \cdot \mathrm{HSF}_3\mathrm{O}. $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.300,r=0.240,b=0.318), with ID f9d46d80-fea4-4f36-b56e-2ed433d85fac -->

We introduce the fraction of free HSF, both in monomer and trimer forms, in homeostasis at T = 37°C: <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.322,r=0.481,b=0.351), with ID 65531cad-6983-4fac-8b73-f36a980cdd52 -->

$ \epsilon_{\mathrm{HSF}} := \mathrm{HSF}_{\mathrm{tot}}^{\mathrm{free}} / \mathrm{HSF}_{\mathrm{tot}}. \qquad \text{(C.12.1)} $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.053,t=0.355,r=0.482,b=0.375), with ID c0e43df8-ca58-4992-bcb4-e8814b1aa56a -->

At 37°C most of HSF monomers are bound to HSP and form the
HSP:HSF complexes, whereas only small percentage of HSF remains
as free monomers, or in trimer form Sarge et al. (1993). This brings
us to the assumption that <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.053,t=0.377,r=0.481,b=0.431), with ID 7c3373c1-3c6a-4125-84a7-e9499997257c -->

$ \epsilon_{\mathrm{HSF}} \in (0,\, 0.1), \hspace{7cm} \text{(C.12.2)} $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.435,r=0.481,b=0.456), with ID 9e955e24-760e-403e-a7f7-e0a1e59ef539 -->

Next, we introduce the fraction of free HSF monomers $\beta_\mathrm{HSF}$ and assign the value of HSF0 via <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.459,r=0.481,b=0.487), with ID 5e3465f2-2104-41ad-aeed-f5cabe43b15a -->

$ \mathrm{HSF0} = \beta_{\mathrm{HSF}} \cdot \mathrm{HSF}_{\mathrm{tot}}. \hspace{5cm} \text{(C.12.3)} $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.490,r=0.481,b=0.510), with ID 79dc7a7c-7c6e-49ab-a963-2e664ce2f44b -->

Since the fraction of free HSF monomers is smaller than the fraction of both free HSF monomers and trimers, we have $\beta_\mathrm{HSF} < \epsilon_\mathrm{HSF}$. In consequence, we assume that <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.514,r=0.481,b=0.553), with ID 1d874faa-bc8b-4ca7-bf90-e7354cacb2de -->

$ \beta_{\mathrm{HSF}} \in (0,\, 0.1). \hspace{7cm} \text{(C.12.4)} $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.051,t=0.558,r=0.481,b=0.579), with ID 4a0a0f06-1bbc-4d13-9d3e-9b490a25f1c0 -->

Range of values of the total concentration HSF$_{\text{tot}}$ is given by Eq. (C.18.1). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.051,t=0.582,r=0.482,b=0.610), with ID b6324770-4ec4-4c76-b064-a6d61a2082bf -->

C.13. Fraction of free HSE in homeostasis at 37°C <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.622,r=0.356,b=0.640), with ID 6d28423b-45ee-4c2a-b468-88c5da02e7d8 -->

Experiments suggest that prior to a heat-shock, the HSE region
of the human HSP70 promoter is not occupied by active transcrip-
tion factor HSF$_3$ (Abravaya et al., 1991b). Therefore, we assume
that, for some positive $\epsilon_{\text{HSE:HSF}_3} \ll 1$, <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.051,t=0.650,r=0.481,b=0.708), with ID 5b72aac3-52d2-4e9c-87e3-12ac717786d8 -->

$ \mathrm{HSE}:\mathrm{HSF}_3^0 = \epsilon_{\mathrm{HSE}:\mathrm{HSF}_3} \cdot \mathrm{HSE}_{\mathrm{tot}}. \hspace{4cm} \text{(C.13.1)} $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.711,r=0.481,b=0.731), with ID 19d37601-8290-4c72-a4ce-06ecc8447275 -->

We assume the following bounds for the fraction $\epsilon^{\mathrm{HSE:HSF}_3}$: <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.053,t=0.736,r=0.456,b=0.753), with ID 5909a2ec-e46c-4d52-a597-868266054c31 -->

$ \zeta_{\mathrm{HSE:HSF}_3} \in (0,\,0.1). \hspace{6cm} \text{(C.13.2)} $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.053,t=0.759,r=0.482,b=0.778), with ID c51c24a9-12ac-45bd-8e34-b2e494d90844 -->

The value of the total concentration HSE$_{\text{tot}}$ is given by Eq. (C.19.1). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.053,t=0.782,r=0.480,b=0.798), with ID f395391c-7f6b-4bee-8b1a-5c55ea7ad0a6 -->

C.14. Fraction of free substrate in homeostasis at 37°C ($\epsilon_S$) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.053,t=0.810,r=0.415,b=0.828), with ID 933a0885-4ca1-41a8-bb0e-28fcbda06011 -->

We assume that in homeostasis at 37°C vast majority of S is
titrated by the chaperon HSP molecules. To capture this, we intro-
duce parameter <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.837,r=0.480,b=0.878), with ID 1da8beb3-0751-4585-939e-01aa716d60d0 -->

$\epsilon_S := S0/S_{\text{tot}0}, \hspace{4cm} \text{(C.14.1)}$

where $S_{\text{tot}0} := S0 + \text{HSP}:S0$, and assume that <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.884,r=0.481,b=0.920), with ID c6a8a2d6-6f7e-439d-ab99-9cfbe0274ff6 -->

$ \epsilon_S \in (0,\, 0.1). \hspace{7cm} \text{(C.14.2)} $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.052,t=0.924,r=0.481,b=0.945), with ID 0e8082e7-db07-445b-9f55-073397929538 -->

C.15. Fraction of bound constitutive HSP in homeostasis at 37°C ($\epsilon_{HSC}$) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.069,r=0.899,b=0.100), with ID e5e7d7b4-38a6-4fb7-9134-f2adeb6de8a0 -->

The parameter $\epsilon_{\mathrm{HSC}}$, introduced in Appendix C.8, represents the
fraction of bound constitutive HSP in homeostasis at 37°C. There-
fore, the product $\epsilon_{\mathrm{HSC}} \cdot \mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}}$ represents the amount of bound
constitutive HSP in homeostasis at 37°C. The amount of bound
constitutive HSP cannot exceed the amount of bound HSP, as HSP
represents both heat-inducible and constitutive HSP in the model
(2.1)–(2.12). The bound HSP in (2.1)–(2.12) is the HSP in complexes
with S and HSF. This leads us to the condition <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.109,r=0.933,b=0.215), with ID 62e6aaff-caf9-49e0-92e5-e04e033fd892 -->

$ \epsilon_{\mathrm{HSC}} \cdot \mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}} \;<\; \mathrm{HSP}:\mathrm{HSF}0 + \mathrm{HSP}:\mathrm{S}0 \hspace{3cm} \text{(C.15.1)} $

or, equivalently,

$ \mathrm{Hsp}70_0^{\mathrm{bound}} \; := \; \mathrm{HSP}:\mathrm{HSF}0 + \mathrm{HSP}:\mathrm{S}0 - \epsilon_{\mathrm{HSC}} \cdot \mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}} \; > \; 0. \hspace{3cm} \text{(C.15.2)} $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.220,r=0.934,b=0.298), with ID b51217b1-aa15-4714-8106-e5f5344590bd -->

In optimization, we keep this condition as a nonlinear constraint.
In addition, using (C.15.1), we set a direct estimate for the range of
the values of $\epsilon_{\text{HSC}}$: <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.505,t=0.302,r=0.934,b=0.344), with ID fe7e46b4-0097-4cf5-9f5d-5ff0d0c71dde -->

$\epsilon^{\mathrm{HSC}} < \frac{\mathrm{HSP}:\mathrm{HSF}0 + \mathrm{HSP}:\mathrm{SO}}{\mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}}} < \frac{\mathrm{HSF}_{\mathrm{tot}} + \mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{tot}}0}{\mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}}},$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.346,r=0.831,b=0.380), with ID 197f5d9e-04e6-4e16-9de1-f5a268fe7369 -->

where $S_{\text{tot}0} := S0 + \text{HSP}:S0$ and $\text{HSF}_{\text{tot}}$ is defined in (3.12). In homeostasis at physiological temperature, the total amount of substrate is low. We capture this by assuming that $S_{\text{tot}0} < \text{HSF}_{\text{tot}}$, which allows to process the above estimate for $\epsilon_{\text{HSC}}$ further: <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.380,r=0.932,b=0.436), with ID a14ec07e-5824-451a-a0ed-7bca3ed840a5 -->

$\epsilon^{\mathrm{HSC}} < \frac{2 \cdot \mathrm{HSF}_{\mathrm{tot}}}{\mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}}}.$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.505,t=0.436,r=0.641,b=0.469), with ID 18436993-7897-4015-8c05-2ef0ebf7e148 -->

Using the extremal values for HSF$_{\text{tot}}$ and HSC$_{\text{tot}}$ assumed in (C.18.1) and (C.20.3), respectively, we get <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.505,t=0.471,r=0.932,b=0.500), with ID 0111435e-c4e5-4bc7-831e-5581786d7c36 -->

$ \epsilon_{\mathrm{HSC}} \in (0,\, 0.004123)\quad \text{unitless.} \hspace{5cm} (\mathrm{C}.15.3) $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.505,t=0.503,r=0.933,b=0.522), with ID 2e9daed5-7808-478b-a5c6-17a90ad3e45c -->

The conditions (C.15.1) or (C.15.2) are more precise than (C.15.3), but (C.15.3) is helpful in optimization for setting box constraints for the parameter $\epsilon_{\mathrm{HSC}}$. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.503,t=0.524,r=0.933,b=0.565), with ID 0760738b-afaf-4727-b866-592b86c305f3 -->

C.16. Ratio of total amounts of heat-inducible and constitutive HSP in
homeostasis at 37°C ($\beta_{HSP}$) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.577,r=0.932,b=0.607), with ID 9137b8bc-86e5-4d81-8b54-bb2d94ba0d9a -->

We introduce the ratio of total amount of heat-inducible and constitutive HSP in homeostasis at 37°C: <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.505,t=0.617,r=0.932,b=0.645), with ID 2f480e98-9c85-44fb-a6a2-bad68d5760c3 -->

$\beta_{\mathrm{HSP}} := \frac{\mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}}}{\mathrm{Hsp}70_{\mathrm{tot}}^0}, \qquad \text{(C.16.1)}$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.505,t=0.649,r=0.933,b=0.678), with ID 451596b2-30f0-4e2f-a680-892f54c1cc76 -->

where the parameter HSC\(_{tot}\), introduced in Appendix C.8, represents the total amount of constitutive HSP and Hsp70\(_{tot}\) is defined by <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.681,r=0.933,b=0.721), with ID 80abebfe-e778-4502-9131-72fb56b9e7d9 -->

$ \mathrm{Hsp}70_{\mathrm{tot}} t := \mathrm{HSP}_{\mathrm{tot}} t - \mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}}, $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.505,t=0.726,r=0.712,b=0.744), with ID af97c458-0d86-432b-b411-bca79c5f657c -->

where HSP$_{tot}$ is defined as in (C.20.1). We assume the reference
value for $\beta_{HSP}$ basing on reference values for HSP$_{tot0}$ and HSC$_{tot}$
discussed in Appendix C.20. Substituting these values above yields
the reference value for $\beta_{HSP}$ equal to 4.8521. Arbitrarily assuming
a twofold error, we obtain <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.747,r=0.932,b=0.814), with ID a3d3e701-1e8a-4728-a897-95d4390cd5ab -->

$\beta_{\mathrm{HSP}} \in (0.5 \cdot 4.8521,\, 2.0 \cdot 4.8521)\ \text{unitless.} \hspace{3cm} (\mathrm{C}.16.2)$ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.818,r=0.933,b=0.839), with ID 574993fb-a286-429f-b4c4-bd5267585d59 -->

C.17. Total amount of denaturation-susceptible proteins ($P_{tot}$) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.505,t=0.847,r=0.879,b=0.865), with ID 00799776-128c-4bd7-b7f0-fa369cb486db -->

The total number of polypeptide molecules per HeLa cell can
be estimated as 2.3·10⁹ (Finka and Goloubinoff, 2013). We use
this value as an estimate for the reference number of protein
molecules per HeLa cell, which, by the conversion (C.0.1), turns
into 1533.3 μM. In the definition of the rate constant $k_{1}^{TI}$ of <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.873,r=0.934,b=0.943), with ID e2fd1fad-df15-4cf7-982c-60bf8aa8d55b -->

G. Dudziuk, W. Wronowska and A. Gambin et al./Journal of Theoretical Biology 478 (2019) 74–101 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.265,t=0.040,r=0.753,b=0.057), with ID 80a84d1f-6165-470e-bca0-97d65d904041 -->

93 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.928,t=0.042,r=0.950,b=0.056), with ID a8103d7d-76cd-4534-8546-3f3c61ace415 -->

misfolding of native proteins P, see Section 2, we have used a
model of the fraction of denatured proteins, $V_{\text{den}}^{\text{T}}$, chosen by com-
parison to experimental data on protein denaturation in the tem-
perature range 30–100 °C, see Appendix A. Therefore, in the model
(2.1)–(2.12), P represents the native proteins susceptible to de-
naturation in the 30–100 °C range, and $P_{\text{tot}}$, defined in (3.11),
represents their total amount. We assume that a vast majority of
cellular proteins is subject to denaturation in 100 °C, or even be-
low (Lepock et al., 1993, Figure 2) and thus the total amount of
denaturation-susceptible proteins $P_{\text{tot}}$ may be approximated by the
total amount of all proteins, with the above given reference value.
Assuming a twofold error for that value, we obtain

$P_{\text{tot}} \in (0.5 \cdot 1533.3, 2.0 \cdot 1533.3) \ \mu \text{M}.$  (C.17.1) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.066,t=0.068,r=0.500,b=0.253), with ID d853a958-565d-4f3c-8029-398ac470a1ec -->

_C.18. Total amount of HSF (HSF$_{tot}$)_

Unstressed human HeLa cells contain approximately $3 \cdot 10^4 \pm 0.33 \cdot 10^4$ molecules of HSF1 (Sarge et al., 1993) ($\pm$ stands for the standard deviation). After a 120 min, 42°C heat-shock, the level of HSF1 in HeLa cells does not significantly change from the level in unstressed cells, however, it has much more narrow distribution ($3 \cdot 10^4 \pm 0.04 \cdot 10^4$ molecules; Sarge et al. (1993)). We take the standard deviation $0.33 \cdot 10^4$, the less restrictive one of the above, into further consideration. Assuming the two-sigma rule to give an interval certain enough, we set

HSF$_{tot} \in (3 \cdot 10^4 - 0.66 \cdot 10^4, 3 \cdot 10^4 + 0.66 \cdot 10^4)$ molecules

which, by application of the conversion (C.0.1), turns into

HSF$_{tot} \in (0.0156, 0.0244)$ $\mu$M.  (C.18.1) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.066,t=0.260,r=0.499,b=0.473), with ID b442c632-a8fc-4456-898f-48c31904b3b3 -->

_C.19. Total amount of HSE (HSE$_{tot}$)_

There are 17 known genes which encode at least one functional
isoform of the 70 kDa HSP family protein (Brocchieri et al., 2008),
out of which only three encode the heat-inducible Hsp70 (HSPA1A,
HSPA1B and HSPA1L, cf. Hageman et al. (2011)). We assume that
both homologous chromosome copies for each of these genes are
active. This results in a total of HSE$_{tot}$ = 6 molecules of HSE. Ap-
plying the conversion (C.0.1), we get <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.068,t=0.478,r=0.497,b=0.602), with ID cf938a9d-8dd8-448b-acdf-debdd675f474 -->

$ \mathrm{HSE}_{\mathrm{tot}} = 4 \cdot 10^{-6} \ \mu \mathrm{M}. \hspace{5cm} (\mathrm{C}.19.1) $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.069,t=0.603,r=0.497,b=0.626), with ID 0bd69f86-e6c9-4150-be2f-d86467f51553 -->

*C.20. Total amount of HSP in homeostasis at 37°C (HSP<sub>tot0</sub> and HSC<sub>tot</sub>)* <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.069,t=0.634,r=0.462,b=0.663), with ID 196f478e-1c14-4327-9f2a-4a489448cf4a -->

We now describe estimates for the total HSP concentration in
homeostasis at 37°C, HSP$_{\text{tot}0}$, and the total constitutive HSP con-
centration, HSC$_{\text{tot}}$. In terms of the variables of the ODE model
(3.1)–(3.10), we define HSP$_{\text{tot}}$ by <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.069,t=0.673,r=0.497,b=0.728), with ID 6a4353a3-c8e6-41f4-b11b-5ed3d19874a6 -->

$ \mathrm{HSP}_{\mathrm{tot}} := \mathrm{HSPt} + \mathrm{HSP:St} + \mathrm{HSP:HSFt}. \hspace{3cm} \mathrm{(C.20.1)} $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.069,t=0.731,r=0.497,b=0.750), with ID 5c5e6704-a923-4634-885e-fe211446b9b6 -->

The model (3.1)–(3.10) does not describe explicitly the dynamics of
constitutive HSP and thus HSC_tot has no straightforward representation in terms of the model variables. We rather treat HSC_tot as a model parameter, as discussed in Appendix C.8. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.070,t=0.754,r=0.497,b=0.806), with ID 4df3c2bb-ab4c-4d8b-bd65-66e78d315d34 -->

To estimate HSP\(_{tot0}\) and HSC\(_{tot}\), we have used Supplementary data by Finka and Goloubinoff ([Finka and Goloubinoff, 2013, Table 3](https://)), which presents estimated numbers of molecules for many chaperome members in HeLa cells, in division w.r.t. cellular compartments. We focused on the data concerning the cytosol and nucleus compartment (CN), the compartments in which the process included in the model takes place. From the CN data, we extracted the data concerning HSP genes. In the extracted data, top 5 entries with highest numbers of molecules form over 90% of <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.070,t=0.807,r=0.497,b=0.925), with ID 9426d9ea-7955-4775-9683-e1b2b1429d4a -->

the total HSP molecules number. The subject top 5 entries con-
cern Hsp90 (HSP90AB1 and HSP90AA1 genes), Hsp70 (HSPA1A/B),
Hsc70 (HSPA8/HSC70) and Hsp27 (HSPB1/HSP27) proteins. How-
ever, in contrary to Hsp90, Hsp70 and Hsc70, which interact with
HSF via its sequestration, the pattern of Hsp27 and HSF interac-
tions described in literature is different (Hsp27 regulates HSF ac-
tivity via sumoiIation; Hsp27 expression does not affect HSF phos-
phorylation or DNA-binding ability (Brunet Simioni et al., 2009)).
Based on this, we assume that Hsp27 does not sequester HSF and
thus does not contribute to the fundamental regulation mechanism
of the biochemical model (2.1)-(2.12). Hence, we omit the amount
of Hsp27 in our estimates. We take the sum of molecules numbers
in the remaining 4 entries, equal to 42 822 845 after rounding, as a
reference value for HSPtot0. Among the proteins in the considered
4 entries, we assume that Hsp90 and Hsc70 are constitutive heat
shock proteins (see the discussion in Section 2) and the reference
value for HSCtot is the sum of their numbers of molecules, which
is 35 505 364 after rounding. Applying the conversion (C.01) to the
reference numbers of molecules and assuming a twofold error, we

$ \mathrm{HSP}_{\mathrm{tot}0} \in (0.5 \cdot 28.5486,\, 2.0 \cdot 28.5486)\ \mu\mathrm{M}, \qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\q $ \mathrm{HSC}_{\mathrm{tot}} \in (0.5 \cdot 23.6702,\, 2.0 \cdot 23.6702)\ \mu\mathrm{M}. \qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad\qquad (\mathrm{C}.20.3) $ <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.522,t=0.371,r=0.948,b=0.391), with ID 846b73f3-73e6-4f0a-938a-118333a5e75c -->

Appendix D. Supplementary results for the fitted model

In this section we provide figures presenting both full results of model calibration (Fig. D.1) and selected model predictions (Figs. D.3–D.6). For the purpose of fitting the ODE model (3.1)–(3.10) to data, we used 19 data series containing jointly 127 measurements, summarized in Table 2 and depicted along with the calibrated model curves in Fig. D.1. We obtained a good fit for almost all data series, however for 2 data series concerning HSE:HSF₃ levels we observed a gap between the calibrated model and the data. We below discuss the fit for these data series in more detail. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.521,t=0.411,r=0.949,b=0.556), with ID 3d5656fd-9957-47dd-8c04-431dad00018c -->

Our model captures correctly the data concerning transcriptional complexes HSE:HSF3 under continuous heat shock, as shown in the 1st row of Fig. D.1. However, it is to be noted that the model fails to capture the dynamics of HSE:HSF3 upon a discontinuous heat shock, responding with too fast recovery after a 40 min treatment at 42 °C, see 2nd and 4th panel in the 2nd row of Fig. D.1. To investigate this in detail, recall that the total amount of HSE in our model is constant (see (3.13)) and therefore the dynamics of HSE:HSF3 depend on changes in the total level of HSF3 and on the reaction rate constants of HSE:HSF3 association and dissociation. To evaluate the impact of HSF3 dynamics on the fast recovery of HSE:HSF3, we performed the following test. We presumed the model for 40 min in a 42 °C heat shock and used the resulting values of HSF3, HSE and HSE:HSF3 as initial conditions to simulate the reaction (2.2) isolated from the model (2.1)-(2.12) at 37 °C. This way we mimicked the recovery of HSE:HSF3 complexes with constant total amount of HSF3. It turned out that the recovery speed of HSE:HSF3 in the isolated reaction (2.2) and in the whole model (2.1)-(2.12) was very similar (data not shown), i.e. in both cases too fast to capture the experimental data. This suggests that fast recovery of HSE:HSF3 is driven mainly by the reaction rate constants of HSE:HSF3 association and dissociation, $k_7$ and $l_7$. On manually modifying the estimated values of $k_7$ and $l_7$ we were able to improve the fit of our HSR model to the recovery data for HSE:HSF3, see Fig. D.2. However, such modifications affect the remaining model curves, and thus have negative impact on the fit for other data series. Hence, optimizing the discussed shortcoming <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.522,t=0.557,r=0.948,b=0.924), with ID c4fc7926-efc8-4e13-9bc3-bdead6fc73f7 -->

94 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.051,t=0.043,r=0.071,b=0.055), with ID 984d3104-85c4-4c62-b961-7d5cd72fb171 -->

G. Dudziuk, W. Wronowska and A. Gambin et al./Journal of Theoretical Biology 478 (2019) 74–101 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.250,t=0.042,r=0.735,b=0.057), with ID 1a00726a-573b-4e44-b31e-195d906a02c4 -->

Summary : This figure presents a comprehensive model fit to experimental data on the heat shock response in cells, showing time courses of various molecular species (HSF, HSE, Hsp70, mRNA) under different heat shock conditions and from multiple literature sources. The plots compare model predictions (lines) to experimental data (red points with error bars).

line chart:
# Title & Axes :
  • No single overarching title; each subplot is individually titled with data source, figure reference, and heat shock (HS) condition (e.g., "Data: Abravaya'91, Fig. 8A, HS: 41°C").
  • X-axis for all plots: "Time (min)" with tick labels typically at 0, 50, 100, 150, 200, 250.
  • Y-axis varies by subplot, including "HSF3+HSE (μM)", "mRNA (μM)", "Hsp70 (μM)", "HSF3/HSF (μM)", "HSF (μM)", and "k_p * HSF3+HSE (μM)".
  • Y-axis tick labels and ranges differ per subplot, e.g., 0 to 2×10⁻⁶ μM, 0 to 1.5×10⁻⁷ μM, 0 to 2×10⁻⁴ μM, etc.

# Data Points :
  • Each subplot contains:
    – Red data points with vertical error bars (standard deviations).
    – Blue model fit line.
  • Data sources include: Abravaya'91, Theodorakis'87, Mosser'88, Andrews'87, Stege'95, Baler'96.
  • Heat shock conditions vary: 41°C, 42°C, 43°C, 44°C, 45°C, and time durations (e.g., 40 min, 120 min, 15 min).
  • Observables include:
    – HSF3+HSE (total HSF bound to HSE DNA site)
    – mRNA (Hsp70 mRNA)
    – Hsp70 (total Hsp70 protein)
    – HSF3/HSF (ratio)
    – HSF (free HSF)
    – k_p * HSF3+HSE (rate term)
  • Example data points (from visible axis ticks and error bars):
    – HSF3+HSE at 41°C: starts high (~1.5×10⁻⁷ μM), decreases over 200 min.
    – mRNA at 42°C: increases from 0 to ~1.2×10⁻² μM over 200 min.
    – Hsp70 at 44°C: rises from 0 to ~1.5×10⁻¹ μM, peaks, then plateaus.

# Design Encodings :
  • Red circles with vertical error bars for experimental data.
  • Solid blue lines for model predictions.
  • Each subplot labeled with data source, figure reference, and heat shock condition.
  • No log scales or dual axes.
  • No explicit statistical test results shown.

# Distribution & Trends :
  • HSF3+HSE: Generally decreases over time after heat shock, with initial peaks at early time points.
  • mRNA: Increases over time, often approaching a plateau.
  • Hsp70: Increases, sometimes with a lag, then plateaus or slightly decreases.
  • Some plots show rapid initial changes followed by slower dynamics.
  • Error bars indicate variability in experimental measurements.

# Analysis :
  • The model (blue lines) generally captures the qualitative trends of the experimental data (red points), including initial peaks, rises, and plateaus.
  • There is variability in the fit quality across different datasets and observables; some data points deviate from the model, especially at later time points or at higher temperatures.
  • The figure demonstrates the model's ability to reproduce diverse experimental results across multiple studies and heat shock conditions, supporting its validity for describing the heat shock response dynamics.
  • The inclusion of error bars highlights experimental uncertainty, and the model fits fall within these uncertainties for most data points. <!-- figure, from page 0 (l=0.050,t=0.064,r=0.937,b=0.733), with ID 0101a152-0dd9-4162-a948-6ee935d3baf9 -->

The above discussed issue may also be related to the data
interpretation. The data from Figs. 5B and 8 in Abravaya et al.
(1991a), including the recovery series, represent active HSF in
complexes with HSE, which correspond to HSE:HSF₃ in our model.
However, as noted by Sivéry et al. (2016), in the gel shift assays
used in the original experiment in Abravaya et al. (1991a) HSE
serves as a binding probe for—and is in excess to—active HSF.
This is in contrary to the cellular mechanism where active
HSF is in excess to HSE. Therefore, it could be reasonable to
consider an alternative data interpretation in which the quan-
tified measurements from Figs. 5B and 8 in Abravaya et al.
(1991a) correspond to HSF₃—which in our model represents <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.051,t=0.766,r=0.482,b=0.928), with ID 2d89f54c-c675-4d91-a1cd-56519be1e1b0 -->

active HSF—rather than to HSE:HSF₃. Actually, the authors of Sriram et al. (2012) and Sivéry et al. (2016) have already used this kind of interpretation. For further refinements in this respect, we note that the experimental measurements in question depend also on the HSE:HSF₃ dissociation constant. Accordingly, one could consider a data interpretation involving the dependence on the latter parameter. This could be beneficial especially in the case of our model where the HSE:HSF₃ dissociation constant is temperature-dependent. Potentially, the reinterpretation of the data in question could address the indicated issues with the recovery dynamics of HSE:HSF₃. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.504,t=0.767,r=0.933,b=0.913), with ID 908b6234-906d-4f1f-a608-9d536b890c6f -->

Appendix E. Supplementary results for optimization

In this section, we present additional results concerning the optimization procedure described Section 3.2. For the 23 best results of the local optimization, the obtained target function values were similar, see Fig. E.1. In Fig. E.2, we provide boxplots of values of directly optimized parameters for these 23 results, along with the optimization constraints. This complements the content of Table C.1, which presents only the values of the finally selected parameters. Fig. E.2 allows to inspect how frequently the successful optimization results were close to the constraints and provides suggestions which constraints were too tight. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.049,t=0.068,r=0.484,b=0.228), with ID 1a4eebcb-e5de-4f33-9943-7ec1784eedb6 -->

Apparently, the optimized values for the dissociation constants
$K_{d,2}$ and $K_{d,7}^{37^\circ C}$ and the maximal transcription rate constant $k_8^{\max}$
frequently were close to the upper bounds. Moreover, many values
obtained for $I_{7,42}$, $I_{7,43}$ and HSF$_{\text{tot}}$ were close to their lower bounds.
Particularly for $K_{d,7}^{37^\circ C}$ and HSF$_{\text{tot}}$ the adhesion to the constraints
seems pronounced, suggesting that the “true” values of these pa-
rameters may lie outside the assumed range. Note however that on
one hand this suggests a higher value of $K_{d,7}^{37^\circ C}$, i.e. the fast disso-
ciation of HSE:HSF$_3$ at 37$^\circ$C, and on the other hand the fast dis-
sociation of HSE:HSF$_3$ may be related to the problems with repro-
duction of HSE:HSF$_3$ dynamics upon a discontinuous heat shock, <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.051,t=0.229,r=0.482,b=0.377), with ID 833fa648-cf8a-41f3-8368-d92160132fea -->

  • X-axis: "Rank index" (no units), tick labels: 1, 10, 20, 30, 40, 50.

box plots:
# Title & Axes :
  • No explicit overall title; each subplot is labeled with a parameter name on the y-axis: k₂ (log), k₉, Kₐ₇^37°C, I₇,₄₂, I₇,₄₅, Tₖ₈, nV, ε_HSC (log), HSC_tot.
  • X-axes: Each subplot has its own x-axis with tick marks; some axes are labeled as "(log)" indicating a logarithmic scale.
  • X-axis tick labels (examples): ranges vary per subplot, e.g., k₂: -10 to 10, k₉: 0.002 to 0.004, Kₐ₇^37°C: 0.002 to 0.008, I₇,₄₂: 0 to 150, I₇,₄₅: 0 to 150, Tₖ₈: 35 to 50, nV: 0 to 100, ε_HSC: -20 to -5, HSC_tot: 10 to 50.

# Data Points :
  • Each subplot contains:
    – Blue box plot showing interquartile range.
    – Red vertical line for the median.
    – Green dot, possibly indicating the mean.
    – Blue circles representing outliers.
    – Orange vertical lines at the plot boundaries.

# Design Encodings :
  • Blue boxes for IQR, red lines for medians, green dots for means, blue circles for outliers.
  • Some axes are on a logarithmic scale (indicated by "(log)" in k₂ and ε_HSC).
  • Orange vertical lines at the far left and right of each subplot, possibly indicating parameter bounds or prior limits.

# Distribution & Trends :
  • Most parameters show a compact interquartile range with a few outliers.
  • Outliers (blue circles) are present in several parameters, notably k₂, Kₐ₇^37°C, I₇,₄₂, HSC_tot.
  • Medians (red lines) and means (green dots) are generally close, but some parameters show slight skewness.
  • Some parameters (e.g., nV) have distributions clustered near the lower bound.

# Analysis :
  • The figure provides a comparative overview of the spread, central tendency, and outliers for multiple parameters.
  • Most parameters are tightly distributed, but a few (e.g., Kₐ₇^37°C, HSC_tot) show more pronounced outliers.
  • The use of log scales for k₂ and ε_HSC suggests these parameters span several orders of magnitude.
  • The orange boundary lines may indicate prior or plausible parameter limits, with most data falling well within these bounds. <!-- figure, from page 0 (l=0.646,t=0.070,r=0.882,b=0.741), with ID 0e17c226-48ad-4d2b-9d0b-987a6f5b6164 -->
